  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_18050').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_18050').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_18050').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script></div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8345094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15122593802321974077"><br/>A classic piece of Wodehouse silliness, involving Bertie Wooster, his formidable Aunt Dahlia and (of course) Jeeves in a scheme to steal an 18th century cow-creamer during a weekend party at an English country house. <br/><br/>Written in 1939, it also features a would-be fascist dictator of England named Spode, head of an organization called "The Black Shorts" (by the time he started his movement, the shirts had already been taken). <br/><br/>Laugh-out-loud funny. Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3067563311" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12879770195091537587">I think I'm pretty safe when I say that <b>Code of the Woosters</b> is generally considered one of, if not <b>the</b>, best when it comes to a Bertie & Jeeves book.<br/>And for good reason.<br/>It. Is. Hilarious.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1579444901ra/28804943.gif' width='400' height='220' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Wodehouse was really on top of his game when he wrote CotW.<br/>It starts Bertie nixing a cruise that Jeeves wants them to go on, moves on to Aunt Dahlia ordering him to sneer at an antique cow creamer, and somehow ends with our favorite Wooster (as he would say) in the soup.<br/><br/>Roderick Spode (leader of the fascist Bla</span>
  <span id="freeText12879770195091537587" style="display:none">I think I'm pretty safe when I say that <b>Code of the Woosters</b> is generally considered one of, if not <b>the</b>, best when it comes to a Bertie &amp; Jeeves book.<br>And for good reason.<br>It. Is. Hilarious.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1579444901ra/28804943.gif" width="400" height="220" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Wodehouse was really on top of his game when he wrote CotW.<br>It starts Bertie nixing a cruise that Jeeves wants them to go on, moves on to Aunt Dahlia ordering him to sneer at an antique cow creamer, and somehow ends with our favorite Wooster (as he would say) in the soup.<br><br>Roderick Spode (leader of the fascist Black Shorts) loves the <i>drippy</i> Madeline, the daughter of Sir Watkyn Bassett - a judge who once fined Bertie £5 for stealing a policeman's helmet. But Madeline is engaged to Bertie's ridiculous friend, Gussie Fink-Nottle - an idiot obsessed with newts. Oh, and Madeline is under the impression that Bertie is secretly in love with her and plans to marry him if anything goes awry with Gussie.<br>Things happen, because of course they do, and it's Bertie to the rescue.<br>Well, it's really Jeeves to the rescue, and the next thing you know Bertie is agreeing to go on that damn cruise. <br>Because of course he does.<br><br>This is the Gold Standard for Jeeves' fans, so if you haven't read it yet, make it a priority.</span>

<div id="review_1978444793" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1978444793" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1978444793?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1978444793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14068665390498646000"><b>Review updated on January, 4 2020.</b><br/><br/><i>"I mean, imagine how some unfortunate Master Criminal would feel, on coming down to do a murder at the old Grange, if he found that not only was Sherlock Holmes putting in the weekend there, but Hercule Poirot, as well."</i><br/><br/>I love this quote. It came from this book. <br/><br/>For those who somehow manage to avoid knowing anything about this classic British humor series the main heroes are Bertie Wooster and his servant Jeeves. Bertie's job is to get into all kind of absurd an</span>
  <span id="freeText14068665390498646000" style="display:none"><b>Review updated on January, 4 2020.</b><br><br><i>"I mean, imagine how some unfortunate Master Criminal would feel, on coming down to do a murder at the old Grange, if he found that not only was Sherlock Holmes putting in the weekend there, but Hercule Poirot, as well."</i><br><br>I love this quote. It came from this book. <br><br>For those who somehow manage to avoid knowing anything about this classic British humor series the main heroes are Bertie Wooster and his servant Jeeves. Bertie's job is to get into all kind of absurd and improbable (from the normal logic point of view) situations arising from his noble attempts to help his two aunts and countless friends and Jeeves' job is to get him out of yet another trouble using some truly ingenious tricks. <br><br>This time it all started very much innocently. Bertie suffering from a hangover from a previous night's party visited one of his aunts - Dahlia. She asked him to go to an antique dealer to pick up a cow-creamer for her husband - an antique collector. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493772073i/22651121._SX540_.jpg" alt="Cow-Creamer" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>At this point I have to talk about this particular kitchen utensil. A cow-creamer is a creamer (a vessel to serve cream in, for example for tea) made of silver in form of a cow; according to the description and Bertie's impression very ugly. I will let Bertie himself describe it.<br><i>"It was a silver cow. But when I say 'cow', don't go running away with the idea of some decent, self-respecting cudster such as you may observe loading grass into itself in the nearest meadow. This was a sinister, leering, Underworld sort of animal, the kind that would spit out of the side of its mouth for twopence."</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578186817i/28712135._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="250" alt="Cow Criminal" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>We all know that there is a very fine line between an enthusiastic collector and fanatical one, so seemingly easy mission of Bertie turned out to be very important. <br><br>Bertie managed to fail, completely and promptly. This failure resulted in his visit to a countryside, double, triple, and quadruple blackmail, desperate attempts to help two couples in love overcome all obstacles in their way, equally desperate attempts to stay out of jail, and avoid getting beaten to pulp. May I remind you the centerpiece of all this excitement was a cow-creamer which antiquity was under serious doubt. Absurd and funny situations guaranteed. <br><br>Let me start with good parts. At times the tale was really funny. I laughed out loud several times with one scene being particular good, especially considering this whole scene was played out in Bertie's imagination. So as a humorous book it works - most of the time.<br><br>Speaking about my problems with it, during my first read I thought one of them was entirely my fault. Some scenes in the beginning depend on the knowledge of the previous adventures to be funny. Yours truly jumped from the first book straight to book seven - this one, so some jokes happily flew over my head unnoticed. The second time around I reread the book just for this reason: I recently finished all six previous books and wanted to check if the knowledge of the background would make the book funnier. It did not. <br><br>The following problems did not depend on the knowledge of the previous books. This book strongly reminded me of a joke. A very funny joke with great payoff, but overly long - like it took this whole book to tell this one joke. Before I read the previous book (more details on it below) I thought that the short story format works much better for Bertie Wooster and Jeeves. Book 6 proved otherwise.<br><br>I was not much impressed with Jeeves' solutions for the impossible situations. They seemed to me sort of ordinary; nothing like a brilliant work of mind of Jeeves in the second short story of the first anthology. That one made me an instant fan of the guy. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493772428i/22651149.jpg" width="460" height="280" alt="Ask Jeeves" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I compared the quotes from the previous book, Right Ho, Jeeves which I consider to be the funniest of the series so far with this one. The former has more brilliant ones, so my initial opinion is still valid: when it comes to humor, book 7 (this one) is not quite on the level with book 6.<br><br>So the final conclusion: good and at times funny one, but its length became its problem - 3 stars. I still want to continue reading the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14068665390498646000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14513759" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14513759" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14513759?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=pants-shittingly-awesome">pants-shittingly-awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=wodehouse">wodehouse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14513759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6428526778023831897">No one weaves a plot like Wodehouse. Also, if you have a cow creamer, guard it with your life.<br/><br/><b>The 2012 re-read:</b><br/>Aunt Dahlia dispatches Bertie to Totleigh Towers to purlorn a silver cow creamer coveted by his uncle Tom from Sir Watkyn Basset. Unfortunately, Bertie has his work cut out for him in the form of Stiffy Byng and Madeline Basset. Can Bertie escape with the cow creamer without winding up married to either woman?<br/><br/>This is my second reading of Code of the Woosters and I can definitely say the</span>
  <span id="freeText6428526778023831897" style="display:none">No one weaves a plot like Wodehouse. Also, if you have a cow creamer, guard it with your life.<br /><br /><b>The 2012 re-read:</b><br />Aunt Dahlia dispatches Bertie to Totleigh Towers to purlorn a silver cow creamer coveted by his uncle Tom from Sir Watkyn Basset. Unfortunately, Bertie has his work cut out for him in the form of Stiffy Byng and Madeline Basset. Can Bertie escape with the cow creamer without winding up married to either woman?<br /><br />This is my second reading of Code of the Woosters and I can definitely say there is a reason I've been recommending it to people for the better part of a decade. P.G. Wodehouse was in mid-season form when he chiseled this masterpiece out of a block of stone. The Code of the Woosters should be handed out in writing classes as a prime example of how to orchestrate a plot. The twists are perfectly timed so the jaw-droppingest moments happen at the end of chapters.<br /><br />The writing is superb and Wodehouse moves his characters through the scenery like a master puppeteer. Gussie Fink-Nottle, that "ghastly gob of gorgonzola," makes his return, still bethrothed(ish) to Madeline Basset and is just as quirky. Who else would think to put newts in the bathtub after breaking an aquariam? La Basset is the same as she was in the previous volume. I'm not sure if Stiffy Byng or Stinker Pinker make appearances in other volumes but they are quite memorable here. Roderick Spode is by far the best supporting character of the book, though, a facist who cowers whenever someone mentions "Eulalie," the meaning of which is not clear until the end. As always, the narrative is a minefield of hilarious similes. <br /><br />The plot meanders all over Totleigh Towers. Like most Jeeves stories, Bertie gets himself deeper and deeper into the soup, the plot encircling such props as the aforementioned cow creamer, a notebook, and a policeman's helmet. As I mentioned before, the reversals of fortune are impecably timed.<br /><br />I could go on and on about this book. Suffice to say, it's an easy five and my go-to recommendation for people who want to give P.G. Wodehouse a shot. They didn't make an episode of the phenomenal BBC Jeeves and Woosters series starring Stephen Fry and Hugh Laurie out of it for nothing!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6428526778023831897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer102828162820630911">There may be no better cure for pandemic blues than P.G. Wodehouse’s incandescently funny prose. Reading Jeeves and Wooster is like snorting unicorn dust; not only does it energize you and make you feel giggly, but it also gets you kind of horny. (I mean like growing a unicorn horn, you pervs—Jeeves and Wooster do not, as a rule, arouse one sexually, though if one is turned on by hilarious Edwardian slang, then one might need to confine one’s reading of these books to one’s boudoir.) <br/><br/>What’s part</span>
  <span id="freeText102828162820630911" style="display:none">There may be no better cure for pandemic blues than P.G. Wodehouse’s incandescently funny prose. Reading Jeeves and Wooster is like snorting unicorn dust; not only does it energize you and make you feel giggly, but it also gets you kind of horny. (I mean like growing a unicorn horn, you pervs—Jeeves and Wooster do not, as a rule, arouse one sexually, though if one is turned on by hilarious Edwardian slang, then one might need to confine one’s reading of these books to one’s boudoir.) <br /><br />What’s particularly delightful about this J&amp;W story is that it’s a full-length affair, so it takes all of the comedic misunderstandings, coincidences, and instances of impossibly bad timing in J&amp;W short stories and amplifies them by 10, with Wodehouse conducting the whole thing like a puppeteer, pulling strings at just the right moment to make you think, “Oh, this can’t possibly go…aaaaahhh! It did! It did go in that direction!”<br /><br />I really can’t recommend these books highly enough. They legitimately make your soul happy.<br /><br />(And, many thanks to the good folks at the legendary Powell’s Books in Portland for shipping me an <a href="https://twitter.com/Gibknight/status/1261077973962313729" rel="nofollow noopener">essential care package</a> this week.)</span>

<div id="review_40431963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40431963" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40431963?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=wodehouses">wodehouses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40431963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17469256330920129133">Classic Wodehouse. It doesn't get any better than this...actually it doesn't get much different than this either. <br/><br/>Perhaps that's not entirely fair. For me at least, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> contains some of my favorite scenes and some of Wodehouse's most memorable characters. Herein his hero Bertie Wooster is at his daffiest, unable to accomplish the simplest of tasks, berating a cow creamer, without getting himself in thick soup. Soon after he's got a Bassett and that malodorous Spode badgering</span>
  <span id="freeText17469256330920129133" style="display:none">Classic Wodehouse. It doesn't get any better than this...actually it doesn't get much different than this either. <br /><br />Perhaps that's not entirely fair. For me at least, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> contains some of my favorite scenes and some of Wodehouse's most memorable characters. Herein his hero Bertie Wooster is at his daffiest, unable to accomplish the simplest of tasks, berating a cow creamer, without getting himself in thick soup. Soon after he's got a Bassett and that malodorous Spode badgering him to no end, and this is hot off the heels of a binge to-do in honor of his fish-faced friend Gussie Fink-Nottle, the newt fancier. Everything seems to converge upon poor Bertie in a most pitiless way, providing the reader with hoots galore and good old fashioned British hijinks. <br /><br />To go back to my original statement...<i>The Code of the Woosters</i>, while a good 'un, is not a vast departure from the normal. Book after book Wodehouse churned out pretty much the same story. But it matters not a lick! The sense of humor might put the starch up some people's collars, but it fits me like a worn-in pair of loafers. Not every book's a school prize winner, but I've seldom been disappointed. If you want to give Wodehouse a go, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> is the stuff to give the troops! </span>
  <a data-text-id="17469256330920129133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2264901619" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2264901619" />
    <a title="Ailsa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6117156-ailsa"><img alt="Ailsa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442286271p2/6117156.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2264901619?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ailsa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ailsa" href="/user/show/6117156-ailsa">Ailsa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2264901619" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16403424925703791828"><b>"We must say to ourselves: "What would Napoleon have done?" He was the boy in a crisis. He knew his onions."</b><br/><br/>Long have I resisted the fatal charm of P.G. Wodehouse. My previous forays into his oeuvre have been lacklustre. That was until, of course, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> and I crossed paths. <br/><br/><b>"He paused and swallowed convulsively, like a Pekingese taking a pill."</b><br/><br/>So scrumptious. I should of known that I would of fallen into the trap sooner or later, given my proclivity for novels of a certain kin</span>
  <span id="freeText16403424925703791828" style="display:none"><b>"We must say to ourselves: "What would Napoleon have done?" He was the boy in a crisis. He knew his onions."</b><br /><br />Long have I resisted the fatal charm of P.G. Wodehouse. My previous forays into his oeuvre have been lacklustre. That was until, of course, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> and I crossed paths. <br /><br /><b>"He paused and swallowed convulsively, like a Pekingese taking a pill."</b><br /><br />So scrumptious. I should of known that I would of fallen into the trap sooner or later, given my proclivity for novels of a certain kind: you know, the jolly-hockey-sticks country house in Surrey à la Nancy Mitford, Evelyn Waugh and Stella Gibbons. <br /><br /><b>"One doesn't want to make a song and dance about one's ancient lineage, of course, but after all the Woosters did come over with the conqueror and were extremely pally with him."</b><br /><br />I am afraid I have become a fan and will be keeping my eyes well and truly peeled for more Jeeves in Wooster in the future. Heartily recommend this instalment to those who think they don't like P.G. Wodehouse. (Spoiler: you do) <br /><br /><b>"She was fully aware that she was doing something that even by female standards was raw, but she didn't care."</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="16403424925703791828" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_15457833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15457833" />
    <a title="Sriram" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/909864-sriram"><img alt="Sriram" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15457833?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sriram" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sriram" href="/user/show/909864-sriram">Sriram</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15457833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12583750251822356118">I'm always shocked to find that hardly anyone in these United States has ever heard of or read the works of Sir Pelham Grenville Wodehouse.... most indians who read english stumble upon his works sooner or later-more so, I dare say, than the english themselves.<br/><br/>I've always maintained that if a Wodehouse book cannot lift your spirits, you must be pretty close to suicide.<br/><br/>Amazing plots, memorable characters and superb prose. Long winding complex sentences that never fail to incite peals of laughter,</span>
  <span id="freeText12583750251822356118" style="display:none">I'm always shocked to find that hardly anyone in these United States has ever heard of or read the works of Sir Pelham Grenville Wodehouse.... most indians who read english stumble upon his works sooner or later-more so, I dare say, than the english themselves.<br /><br />I've always maintained that if a Wodehouse book cannot lift your spirits, you must be pretty close to suicide.<br /><br />Amazing plots, memorable characters and superb prose. Long winding complex sentences that never fail to incite peals of laughter, long after you've put the book down.<br /><br />Wodehouse is the master of the simile and the metaphor.<br /><br />He can nail a character with just one sentence. His stories are full of estranged lovers, funny misunderstandings, meddlesome aunts, young curates ambitious vicars and absent minded uncles and an absolutely convoluted plot.<br /><br />Bertram Wilberforce Wooster and his "Gentleman's personal gentleman"-the incomparable Reginald Jeeves will remain of of the most memorable characters in literature.<br /><br /><br />Quotes like this will be peppered throughout the book<br /><br />"It was a confusion of ideas between him and one of the lions he was hunting in Kenya that had caused A. B. Spottsworth to make the obituary column. He thought the lion was dead, and the lion thought it wasn't."<br /><br />"I could see that, if not actually disgruntled, he was far from being gruntled. "<br /><br />"Memories are like mulligatawny soup in a cheap restaurant. It is best not to stir them. "<br /><br /><br />QED.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12583750251822356118" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_24042316" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24042316" />
    <a title="Alison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/124482-alison"><img alt="Alison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517201678p2/124482.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24042316?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alison" href="/user/show/124482-alison">Alison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">looking for light, clever fun</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Alison by: </span>
      Morfeus
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/124482-alison?shelf=rgbookclub">rgbookclub</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24042316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12955263428557669954">"You might put it that Hell's foundations are quivering. That is not overstating it, Jeeves?"<br/>"No, sir."<br/><br/>P.G. Wodehouse was an English writer whose career spanned over seventy years and whose work included almost 100 novels, numerous short stories, 15 plays and 250 lyrics for some thirty musical comedies. Despite his impressive resume and reputation as a "master of English prose", I, unfortunately, wouldn't have known he existed if it hadn't been for Rory Gilmore. I guess that makes me more of a B</span>
  <span id="freeText12955263428557669954" style="display:none">"You might put it that Hell's foundations are quivering. That is not overstating it, Jeeves?"<br />"No, sir."<br /><br />P.G. Wodehouse was an English writer whose career spanned over seventy years and whose work included almost 100 novels, numerous short stories, 15 plays and 250 lyrics for some thirty musical comedies. Despite his impressive resume and reputation as a "master of English prose", I, unfortunately, wouldn't have known he existed if it hadn't been for Rory Gilmore. I guess that makes me more of a Bertie than a Jeeves.<br /><br />Many of his works include recurring settings and characters. One such set of characters, Bertram Wooster and his clever butler Jeeves, are at the heart of "The Code of the Woosters." "Bertie" is an old school English bachelor who rubs elbows with other upper-class British socialites. He's a bit dim yet extremely loyal (it's part of his own moral *code*), and to say that he has a knack for getting himself into <i>situations</i> is an understatement. Jeeves, his loyal servant, is there to get Bertie out of his entanglements by use of his good sense and keen problem-solving skills. (He's so good with information, he even had a search engine named after him--Ask.com was previously AskJeeves.com).<br /><br /><br />Throughout reading, I was trying to decide how I would best describe Wodehouse's style. The best I could come up with was "Sherlock Holmes meets Three's Company." There are no mysteries here...no murders or missing person cases to be solved. But each particular mess Bertie finds himself in ("the spectacle of as raw a bit of underhanded skulduggery as has ever been perpetrated in a civilized country") requires as much instinct and "know-how" to tidy up as any case Holmes was called in on. The predicaments seem to follow the Three's Company philosphy of situational comedy: If it <i>can</i> happen, it <i>will</i>...and with the most inconvenient timing, ironically, and with things and body parts getting bruised and broken. Throw in some dry, deapan dialogue, a lot of black-mailing, and multiply the shenanigans by ten. <br /><br />His descriptions are some of the best and most comical ever written...<br /><br />On Gussie: "a fish-faced pal", "confirmed recluse", "a less promising prospect (for marriage) it would have seemed impossible to discover in a month of Sundays"<br /><br />On Madeline Bassett: "a droopy, soupy, sentimental exhibit, with melting eyes and a cooing voice and the most extraordinary views on such things as stars and rabbits"<br /><br />On Spode: "It was as if Nature had intended to make a gorilla and had changed its mind at the last moment"<br /><br />I can see myself being hooked on this series. It's light, it's intelligent, it's clever and engaging...as I am reading, I'm smiling inside.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12955263428557669954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1964239389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8293629165238781451"><b> “It’s an extraordinary thing—every time I see you, you appear to be recovering from some debauch. Don’t you ever stop drinking? How about when you are asleep?” </b><br/><br/>As usual, I'm behind on reading goals - being the last to finish this buddy read with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/9340136-evgeny' rel='nofollow noopener'>Evgeny</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/12058788-2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dan 2.0</a><br/><br/>My first foray into Wodehouse’s writing, and I’ve fallen for him. The comical and cleverly coined style made this one a fun read, even if the plot only left behind a three-star impression.<br/><br/>I finally see where the "Jeeves" came from - an</span>
  <span id="freeText8293629165238781451" style="display:none"><b> “It’s an extraordinary thing—every time I see you, you appear to be recovering from some debauch. Don’t you ever stop drinking? How about when you are asleep?” </b><br /><br />As usual, I'm behind on reading goals - being the last to finish this buddy read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/9340136-evgeny" rel="nofollow noopener">Evgeny</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/12058788-2" rel="nofollow noopener">Dan 2.0</a><br /><br />My first foray into Wodehouse’s writing, and I’ve fallen for him. The comical and cleverly coined style made this one a fun read, even if the plot only left behind a three-star impression.<br /><br />I finally see where the "Jeeves" came from - and I approve. The highlights of the story were when Master and butler collaborated, argued, or battled protective guard dogs over bed sheets. Fun times!<br />It’s now gotten my attention that this is the third interesting story I’ve read about this form of relationship, and I’ve enjoyed all three. Need to track down more eventually.<br /><br />Bertie runs into foul circumstances and somewhat annoying misunderstandings when he sets out to make things right for his aunt’s cow creamer goal – only to end up sabotaging a friend’s pending marriage, his possible freedom at the hands of a local magistrate, and bungling his aunt’s cow-creamer dreams and his attachment to her cook’s artistic food that he apparently would almost risk his soul for.<br /><br />Not all characters were likeable – I wanted to shake a particular girl – gah, what a nuisance!<br /><br />Kind of like an episode of Green Acres where it’s funny because so many people are exasperating, but while I can stand a 20 something minute episode of Green Acres, I wouldn’t want to sit down for an extended episode that lasted much longer than that. With this book it carried on a bit too much for my nerves, so it wasn’t all fun and games, but still a classic worth reading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8293629165238781451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3192095442" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3192095442" />
    <a title="Jonathan K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32433113-jonathan-k"><img alt="Jonathan K" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404758116p2/32433113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3192095442?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3192095442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15270733275215737359">For those familiar with the Wooster/Jeeves stories, this is yet another circus with Bertie as ring master. As with all Woodhouse stories, the plot constantly shifts which in turn, requires carefully executed plans, most often created by Bertie's manservant, Jeeves. When Aunt Dahlia comes into the picture, the results are laugh out loud experiences.   <span id="freeText15270733275215737359" style="display:none">For those familiar with the Wooster/Jeeves stories, this is yet another circus with Bertie as ring master. As with all Woodhouse stories, the plot constantly shifts which in turn, requires carefully executed plans, most often created by Bertie's manservant, Jeeves. When Aunt Dahlia comes into the picture, the results are laugh out loud experiences. With a silver 'cow creamer' as the object of attention, Bertie is continually roped into schemes, and as usual, they all backfire. Imagine Lt Clouseau with a British butler and you'd have the makings of the story. What I love about PG Wodehouse are the character names, British phrases (Ha!) and contrived plots all of which are hysterical. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15270733275215737359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_213672133" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/213672133" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213672133?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140009354" href="/book/show/1134032.Code_Of_The_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=humour">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213672133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7267812770341062718">Many consider this book to be the funniest of the Jeeves/ Wooster team, and I agree (though IMO, I would give that crown to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9850375.Right_Ho__Jeeves__Jeeves___6_' title='Right Ho, Jeeves (Jeeves, #6) by P.G. Wodehouse' rel='nofollow noopener'>Right Ho, Jeeves</a>) that it is indeed extremely hilarious. Bertie, saved from the scaffold (i. e. marriage to Madeline Basset who thinks that the stars are God's daisy chain and every time a fairy sheds a tear, a star is born) at the last moment by Jeeves, finds himself ensconced in the country estate of the girl's father along with Gussie Fink-Nottle, her fiancée and Roderic</span>
  <span id="freeText7267812770341062718" style="display:none">Many consider this book to be the funniest of the Jeeves/ Wooster team, and I agree (though IMO, I would give that crown to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9850375.Right_Ho__Jeeves__Jeeves___6_" title="Right Ho, Jeeves (Jeeves, #6) by P.G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">Right Ho, Jeeves</a>) that it is indeed extremely hilarious. Bertie, saved from the scaffold (i. e. marriage to Madeline Basset who thinks that the stars are God's daisy chain and every time a fairy sheds a tear, a star is born) at the last moment by Jeeves, finds himself ensconced in the country estate of the girl's father along with Gussie Fink-Nottle, her fiancée and Roderick Spode (the leader of a fascist organisation built on the lines of a Patton tank), who is also in love with Madeline. To further complicate matters, Madeline's father (who is also the district magistrate) is under the impression that Bertie is a kleptomaniac: Roderick Spode is of the opinion that Gussie Fink-Nottle is a "butterfly who toys with women's hearts and casts them aside like soiled gloves" and therefore wants to "pull him inside out and make him swallow himself". Add to this concoction Stephanie Byng, Madeline's flighty cousin who wants her fiancée, the curate Stephen Pinker, to prove himself by stealing the local constable's helmet - and last but not least, Bertie's Aunt Dahlia who has a mission for him - steal Judge Basset's cow-creamer from his ancient brass collection for Uncle Tom.<br /><br />There are engagements which are broken and made up with alacrity, nightly escapades with Bertie on the run from pursuing females, a murderous would-be dictator and a pissed-off policeman, and crime and intrigue which could prove more than a plateful for Holmes and Poirot put together. In the midst of this all, calm and unruffled, is the figure of Jeeves who sets everything right by threatening to disclose the truth about 'Eulalie'.<br /><br />If you are down in the dumps, this is the book to read. Uncontrollable laughter is guaranteed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7267812770341062718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2377699551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer4009728729334934336">Classic Wodehouse humor with an insane convergence of many plots all converging with Bertie Wooster at their center. Only his man Jeeves has the brain power to extract him unscathed from the perils that threaten over the silver cow creamer, constable's helmet, a brown notebook, and possible engagement to two young ladies. At its best when being read into one's shell-like.<br/><br/>One thing I did notice after all these years is that I now know many more of the half-uttered quotations and references Bertie</span>
  <span id="freeText4009728729334934336" style="display:none">Classic Wodehouse humor with an insane convergence of many plots all converging with Bertie Wooster at their center. Only his man Jeeves has the brain power to extract him unscathed from the perils that threaten over the silver cow creamer, constable's helmet, a brown notebook, and possible engagement to two young ladies. At its best when being read into one's shell-like.<br /><br />One thing I did notice after all these years is that I now know many more of the half-uttered quotations and references Bertie always has to ask Jeeves finish. Decades of reading actually can lead to self-education!</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_292908871" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292908871" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/292908871?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer292908871" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7363354931179710613">Wodehouse loves to pepper his texts with all kinds of wacky similes, so I would like to start my review with one too: reading one of his novels is like drinking a glass of chilled champagne, on a sunny morning, reclining in a chaisez longue on an impeccably trimmmed English lawn. And Code of the Woosters is a Grand Cru - one of the best years.<br/><br/> My previous Wodehouse novels were written in third person, this time Bertie Wooster is the narrator and I noticed an increase in goofiness and general bon</span>
  <span id="freeText7363354931179710613" style="display:none">Wodehouse loves to pepper his texts with all kinds of wacky similes, so I would like to start my review with one too: reading one of his novels is like drinking a glass of chilled champagne, on a sunny morning, reclining in a chaisez longue on an impeccably trimmmed English lawn. And Code of the Woosters is a Grand Cru - one of the best years.<br /><br /> My previous Wodehouse novels were written in third person, this time Bertie Wooster is the narrator and I noticed an increase in goofiness and general bonhomie. A self described boulevardier, he is a well heeled gent with an eye for good food and riotous parties, the only cloud on his horizon being the prospect of marriage. In his own words he feels "hounded like the dickens by a remorseless Fate" every time droopy Madeline announces the break up of her nuptials with Gussy Fink-Nottle.<br /><br />The plot of the Code of the Woosters is best described for me as "screwball" comedy of the kind popular in Hollywood in the 30's. To Romanian readers the reference would be Ion Luca Caragiale and his masterpiece "A Stormy Night" (O Noapte Furtunoasa) . I will once more let Bertie give the introduction:<br /><br /><i> ... Little knowing, as I crossed that threshold, that in about two shakes of a duck's tail I was to become involved in an imbroglio that would test the Wooster soul as it had seldom been tested before. I allude to the sinister affair of Gussie Fink-Nottle, Madeline Bassett, old Pop Bassett, Stiffy Byng, the Rev. H. P. ('Stinker') Pinker, the eighteenth-century cow-creamer and the small, brown, leather-covered notebook </i><br /><br />The scene of the action : Totleigh Towers, a sumptuous country residence in Gloucestershire, where young people in love are struggling to overcome the opposition of parents and uncles and the various misunderstandings Fate chooses to throw in the path of their happyness. The arrival of good intentioned Bertie and suave Jeeves serves to complicate matters instead of providing solutions.<br /><br />The use of the English language, seasoned with the occassional French bon mot or Latin dictum, is as usual superb, and I gladly dived into a dictionary to check if a word is real or invented by the author. I am satisfied with "espieglerie" but my bean is still baffled at "bishing" , "mazzard" or "rannygazoo". To give another example of what constitues a running gag in the story, Stephanie "Stiffy" Byngs is affectionatelly called by Bertie as:<br />- young pill<br />- young squirt<br />- young pimple<br />- young shrimp<br />- young loony (I might have missed a couple)<br /><br />Aunt Dahlia gets a simiar treatment as "old egg", "old flesh and bones" , "old ancestor" and so on, but what cracked me up regarding her is Bertie's rant about aunts in general:<br /><br /><i> 'If I had my life to live again, Jeeves, I would start it as an orphan without any aunts. Don't they put aunts in Turkey in sacks and drop them in the Bosphorus?'<br />'Odalisques, sir, I understand. Not aunts.'<br />'Well, why not aunts? Look at the trouble they cause in the world. I tell you, Jeeves, and you may quote me as saying this - behind every poor, innocent, harmless blighter who is going down for the first time in the soup, you will find, if you look carefully enough, the aunt who shoved him into it.' </i><br /><br />Actually, I have given up extracting quotes from the text when I realized I interrupted my reading almost every page to jot something down. It's that kind of book. Regretfully, I have little chance now to sprinkle my conversations with Bertie's signature exclamations ( "Dash it!" "What ho!" , "Rather!") and the stylish insults of yonder time are also out of style. It's easier to drop a four letter expletive than to call somebody a "ghastly goggle-eyed piece of gorgonzola". The alliteration pointing at Gussie, obviously.<br /><br />Having watched the BBC production of "Jeeves and Wooster" , reading the novel now was a very visual experience, and I feel I should congratulate the casting department for the excellent choices made. All the actors did an excellent job, especially Gussie, Spode and Madeline were spot on with the book.<br /><br />In closing remarks, this is an excellent candidate for a re-read at some point in the future, when I will need a pick me up lecture. Because :<br /><br /><i> Well, this should certainly teach us, should it not, never to repine, never to despair, never to allow the upper lip to unstiffen, but always to remember that, no matter how dark the skies may be, the sun is shining somewhere and will eventually come smiling through. </i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7363354931179710613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169387046" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169387046" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175635-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565151545p2/175635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169387046?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/175635-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169387046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3828155497375863128">I really enjoyed this one. But my favourite joke in the whole thing was when Wooster refers to someone as a ‘sensitive plant’ and is told, “You know your Shelley!” To which he replies, “Am I?” That just about sums up everything I love about Wodehouse. The poetic reference Wooster only uses because he steals it from Jeeves and then his utter bewilderment at what he takes to be a bizarre adjective being used to refer to him. Utter joy.<br/><br/>And the women in this one are even more selfish and manipulativ</span>
  <span id="freeText3828155497375863128" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this one. But my favourite joke in the whole thing was when Wooster refers to someone as a ‘sensitive plant’ and is told, “You know your Shelley!” To which he replies, “Am I?” That just about sums up everything I love about Wodehouse. The poetic reference Wooster only uses because he steals it from Jeeves and then his utter bewilderment at what he takes to be a bizarre adjective being used to refer to him. Utter joy.<br /><br />And the women in this one are even more selfish and manipulative than usual. Wodehouse’s women are a complete joy, a species apart whether aunts or not. <br /><br />I also loved Wodehouse saying, in the darkest before dawn moment, that it would be impossible for all of the ‘nice’ characters in this to get their happy ending. This is something the reader can sympathise with when it is said, and marvel over, knowing even as it is said that somehow the master will untangle the whole mess in satisfyingly perfect fashion – in fact, even with apparent effortlessness.<br /><br />And a fascist even gets bossed around and humiliated by Wooster – someone not known for consistently having it over anyone. Wooster's all-too-human failings with life handing him the wrong end of the stick all being part of the delight these stories offer.<br /><br />Can’t end this review without using the word ‘joy’ for a third time, but that is how I think of these books – something impossible to forget, like a warm smile on a cold afternoon.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3828155497375863128" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3369697358" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3369697358" />
    <a title="Kavita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita"><img alt="Kavita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315387023p2/5858699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3369697358?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kavita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kavita" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita">Kavita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=uk">uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3369697358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4154792299271605006"><i>The Code of the Woosters</i> is as funny as it gets and Wodehouse's masterpiece. The title refers to the fact that the Woosters never let a pal down, which leads Bertie Wooster into many a pretty pickle. When Gussie Fink-Nottle demands his help in healing the breach between himself and his fiancée, Bertie rushes to his help and goes to Totleigh Towers, the sinister den of Sir Watkyn Basset. Meanwhile, a cow creamer is creating trouble for our hero. <br/><br/>This is a brilliant piece of humour and I really do</span>
  <span id="freeText4154792299271605006" style="display:none"><i>The Code of the Woosters</i> is as funny as it gets and Wodehouse's masterpiece. The title refers to the fact that the Woosters never let a pal down, which leads Bertie Wooster into many a pretty pickle. When Gussie Fink-Nottle demands his help in healing the breach between himself and his fiancée, Bertie rushes to his help and goes to Totleigh Towers, the sinister den of Sir Watkyn Basset. Meanwhile, a cow creamer is creating trouble for our hero. <br /><br />This is a brilliant piece of humour and I really don't think even Wodehouse could better it! The characters are brilliant, though many have appeared before. Aunt Dahlia is one of my favourite characters (she doesn't eat broken glass for breakfast, unlike Bertie's Aunt Agatha!). Then there is the really weird Gussie Fink-Nottle, who from his name downwards is pretty silly. The clumsy Harold Pinker, the blackmailing Stephanie Byng all add to the drama. <br /><br />There is even a nod to Oswald Mosley in Roderick Spode. However, because apparently Mosley had taken all the shirts for his blackshirts, Spode had to make do with football shorts! :D The novel was written in 1938 and poked fun at the politics of the time. <br /><br />And of course, the recurring theme of the Jeeves &amp; Wooster series - Jeeves saves the day. For everyone. Several times! What a baa lamb! :D <br /><br />This is one of my favourite books of all time, and this re-read hasn't changed my opinion. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4154792299271605006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2954851249" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2954851249" />
    <a title="Joshie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie"><img alt="Joshie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588954776p2/11973316.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2954851249?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshie" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie">Joshie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=literature-british-and-others">literature-british-and-others</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2954851249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14059020399700815744">“Never let a pal down.”<br/><br/>If someone told me how hilarious <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> was I wouldn’t have believed them with a bat of an eyelash. As someone who doesn’t laugh very easily I’m surprised by the countless of guffaws and snorts I let out as I‌ made my way through this charming novel. P.G. Wodehouse’s prose is easy-breezy with an amusing lyrical clang that bangs with comical twists and ingenious schemes. Wonderfully stitched, it tells of Bertram Wooster and a series of seemingly ridiculous</span>
  <span id="freeText14059020399700815744" style="display:none">“Never let a pal down.”<br /><br />If someone told me how hilarious <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> was I wouldn’t have believed them with a bat of an eyelash. As someone who doesn’t laugh very easily I’m surprised by the countless of guffaws and snorts I let out as I‌ made my way through this charming novel. P.G. Wodehouse’s prose is easy-breezy with an amusing lyrical clang that bangs with comical twists and ingenious schemes. Wonderfully stitched, it tells of Bertram Wooster and a series of seemingly ridiculous mishaps he has to get himself out of with a brilliant helping hand from his cunning manservant Jeeves. With a number of betrothals to fix, a cow-creamer to acquire for a dear aunt, and a stolen policeman helmet to rid of, <i>The Code of the Woosters</i> is a perfect blend of manipulation, silliness, and creative wordplay (fun enough to say out loud whilst reading) without much of a clear conclusion in sight. This builds anticipation really well; the pages end up being consumed quicker than expected then it closes with a big grin of relief. Ah, all’s well that end’s well indeed. <br /><br />(I‌ can’t wait to read my next Wodehouse novel. What the next one will be, I don’t think I’ll make any incorrect choice.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14059020399700815744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2954851249">

<div id="review_49775572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49775572" />
    <a title="F.R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1309606-f-r"><img alt="F.R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544886612p2/1309606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49775572?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="F.R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="F.R." href="/user/show/1309606-f-r">F.R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841591001" href="/book/show/105986.The_Code_of_the_Woosters">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49775572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6316231216182563666"><b> Addendum, January the 3rd 2014</b><br/><br/>Well last night I went to see the West End show: ‘Jeeves & Wooster in Perfect Nonsense’, which is an adaptation of ‘The Code of the Woosters’. My lovely fiancée bought us tickets for Christmas, which truly thrilled me whilst also making me a little anxious. Could any adaptation of what is one of my favourite books live up to my lofty expectations? If it didn’t reach the heights, could I hide my disappointment from my love and thus not seem ungrateful for this wonde</span>
  <span id="freeText6316231216182563666" style="display:none"><b> Addendum, January the 3rd 2014</b><br /><br />Well last night I went to see the West End show: ‘Jeeves &amp; Wooster in Perfect Nonsense’, which is an adaptation of ‘The Code of the Woosters’. My lovely fiancée bought us tickets for Christmas, which truly thrilled me whilst also making me a little anxious. Could any adaptation of what is one of my favourite books live up to my lofty expectations? If it didn’t reach the heights, could I hide my disappointment from my love and thus not seem ungrateful for this wonderful present?<br /><br />I needn’t have worried, I had a whale of a time.<br /><br />Stephen Mangan (from ‘Green Wing’ and ‘Episodes’) is Wooster, with Matthew Macfadyen (who I’ve only ever seen in ‘Ripper Street’ and ‘Frost/Nixon’) is Jeeves. The conceit of the show is that Wooster has been relating his recent adventures at Totleigh Towers to Bingo Little at The Drones, and Bingo has responded by saying it’s so entertaining it should be on the stage. Struck by the brilliance of that idea, Wooster has hired out a theatre and this is that show. Of course Jeeves is there to lend a hand, helping with the scenery and playing himself and other characters; while in addition he has also roped in Aunt Dahlia’s butler, Steppings, to impersonate those who Jeeves can’t himself. And what follows is a comic tour de force.<br /><br />Obviously when you have only three actors playing numerous parts you are really showing off what the theatre can do. And each of the performers is excellent. Mangan is a superbly gormless and constantly cheerful Wooster; while Macfadyen is the perfect and utterly versatile Jeeves. Watching Jeeves pretend to be Sir Watkyn Bassett having a conversation with his niece Stiffy Bing, who Jeeves also happens to be playing in this performance, really does show acting brilliance.(Watching ‘Ripper Street’ one wouldn’t really get the impression that Macfadyen was so funny, or so adept at female impersonation.) The same goes for theatrical actor Mark Hadfield as Steppings, who handles both sides of Aunt Dahlia’s argument with Roderick Spode with aplomb. While the direction and seemless choreography of every set alteration and quick costume change around them is just so exquisite.<br /><br />So, very clever and very funny and technically brilliant, but is it Wodehouse? Well, the answer is a resounding yes. Unlike Hugh Laurie in the TV adaptation, Mangan gets to do a lot of narration, as such Wodehouse’s prose style makes it onto the stage. Obviously it’s in an edited form, but the flavour is there and unmistakeable. And of course the jokes are tried, tested and now absolutely priceless. If I’m honest, the grand telling off Wooster gives Spode (which I quote below) was a bit truncated for my tastes, I would have liked the whole thing delivered gleefully to the rafters. But the fact that we were told before the play even started that if he was to hear a mobile phone device, Mr Wooster would be “not actually disgruntled, but far from being gruntled” more than made up for it and just proved how clever a production this is.<br /><br />It’s apparently on in London until Spring, after which it goes on tour. If you’re a Wodehouse fan (in which case then, of course, you’re a fan of this book), then you needn’t feel any trepidation about purchasing yourself (and maybe your better half) some tickets.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>Review written the 29th of July, 2012</b><br /><br /><i>“I could see that, if not actually disgruntled, he was far from being gruntled.”</i><br /><br /><i>“I mean, imagine how some unfortunate Master Criminal would feel, on coming down to do a murder at the old Grange, if he found that not only was Sherlock Holmes putting in the weekend there, but Hercule Poirot, as well.”</i><br /><br />There was a temptation, for my second review of this novel in what I now term the ‘Goodreads period ‘ of my life, to just illustrate the wonder and genius of this book with quote after quote. The two above, for instance, stand out as a pair of my absolute favourites in the English language, let alone this book. But the problem with that strategy is there are so many good and quotable lines and jokes in ‘The Code of the Woosters’ – some remembered by me, some not; some, the versions of which I had in my head, were only pale shadows of the lines Wodehouse had conjured on the page – that I would be here all day virtually typing the entire text into the computer. And somehow I can’t imagine the good people at the Wodehouse estate being particularly chuffed at that.<br /><br />Quite simply ‘The Code of the Woosters’ is one of the finest comic novels ever written. It stands at the very top of the Wodehouse canon, and thus is one of the funniest books ever written. Our poor, put-upon hero sets off to Totleigh Towers, home of his nemesis Sir Watkin Bassett. There he finds himself pressed upon from many different sides to steal a silver cow creamer (which is most definitely not modern Dutch); in possession of a purloined policeman’s helmet, and attempting to bring various sundered hearts together again. The precision of the writing, the incredible pace and control of the building farce, and the way that every joke is as light as a soufflé, when it has clearly been worked upon so that every syllable is the right place – makes ‘The Code of the Woosters’ an absolute masterpiece.<br /><br />On a quick historical note, this is that rare Wodehouse novel which lets current events intrude into it – with the presence of Roderick Spode. Roderick Spode is of course based upon Sir Oswald Mosley, leader of The British Fascist Party, whose organisation were known as The Black Shirts (changed to ‘shorts’ for comic effect in the book). These days Mosley may be best known in some quarters for his part in the Mitford family saga (he was married to Diana), but in 1938 – when this book was published – was <i>the</i> major force of the British far right and closely aligned with Hitler and Mussolini. After the war Wodehouse’s own allegiances to Great Britain were (somewhat unfairly) called into question, and he never went to his homeland again. As such it’s worthwhile taking a moment to emphasise what he wrote on the likes of Mosley, so here is Bertie’s priceless telling off of Spode:<br /><br /><i>“The trouble with you, Spode, is that just because you have succeeded in inducing a handful of half-wits to disfigure the London scene by going about in black shorts, you think you’re someone. You have them shouting, ‘Heil, Spode!” and you imagine it is the Voice of the People. This is where you make your bloomer. What the Voice of the People is saying is ‘Look at that frightful ass Spode swanking about in footer bags! Did you ever in your puff see such a perfect perisher?”</i><br /><br />Absolutely recommended!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>Review written the 21st of March, 2009</b><br /><br />I love Wodehouse.<br /><br />Even though he writes in a completely different genre (a completely different world even) to me, old P.G. is still one of the authors I draw the most inspiration from. Anyone wanting to improve their own prose should check out Wodehouse. Note the turn of phrase, pay careful attention to the dialogue, marvel at the way the cliche is avoided. Everybody can learn from The Master.<br /><br />This is about the third or fourth time I've read The Code of the Woosters and it remains a joy. I'm not going to go through the plot details here, just say that it is the cream of the cream of the Jeeves and Wooster books, and that J and W are the c of the c of Wodehouse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6316231216182563666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
